<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Parenting</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Process of raising a child</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the magazine, see <a href="/wiki/Parenting_(magazine)" title="Parenting (magazine)">Parenting (magazine)</a>. For parental care in animals, see <a href="/wiki/Parental_investment" title="Parental investment">Parental investment</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baby_Sofia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4032" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Baby_Sofia.jpg/220px-Baby_Sofia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Baby_Sofia.jpg/330px-Baby_Sofia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Baby_Sofia.jpg/440px-Baby_Sofia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baby_Sofia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Father" title="Father">father</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mother" title="Mother">mother</a> hold their child</div></div></div>
<p><b>Parenting</b> or <b>child rearing</b> promotes and supports the <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social</a>, spiritual and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_development" title="Intellectual development">intellectual development</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">child</a> from <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infancy</a> to <a href="/wiki/Adult" title="Adult">adulthood</a>. Parenting refers to the intricacies of raising a child and not exclusively for a biological relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common caretaker in parenting is the father or mother, or both, the <a href="/wiki/Parent#Biological_parents_and_parental_testing" title="Parent">biological parents</a> of the child in question. However, a surrogate parent may be an older sibling, a step-parent, a <a href="/wiki/Grandparent" title="Grandparent">grandparent</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Legal_guardian" title="Legal guardian">legal guardian</a>, aunt, uncle, other family members, or a family friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">Governments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> may also have a role in child-rearing or upbringing. In many cases, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orphaned" title="Orphaned">orphaned</a> or abandoned children receive parental care from non-parent or non-blood relations. Others may be <a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">adopted</a>, raised in <a href="/wiki/Foster_care" title="Foster care">foster care</a>, or placed in an <a href="/wiki/Orphanage" title="Orphanage">orphanage</a>. Parenting skills vary, and a parent or surrogate with good parenting skills may be referred to as a <i>good parent</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HumanEnrich_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-HumanEnrich-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Parenting_styles" title="Parenting styles">Parenting styles</a> vary by historical period, race/ethnicity, social class, preference, and a few other social features.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Additionally, research supports that parental history, both in terms of attachments of varying quality and parental <a href="/wiki/Psychopathology" title="Psychopathology">psychopathology</a>, particularly in the wake of adverse experiences, can strongly influence parental sensitivity and child outcomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Factors_that_affect_decisions">Factors that affect decisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Factors that affect decisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">Social class</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a>, culture and <a href="/wiki/Income" title="Income">income</a> have a very strong impact on what methods of child rearing parents use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Cultural values play a major role in how a parent raises their child. However, parenting is always evolving, as times, cultural practices, social norms, and traditions change. Studies on these factors affecting parenting decisions have shown just that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In psychology, the <a href="/wiki/Parental_investment" title="Parental investment">parental investment</a> theory suggests that basic differences between males and females in parental investment have great adaptive significance and lead to gender differences in mating propensities and preferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Psychology:_Themes_and_Variations_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Psychology:_Themes_and_Variations-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>A family's social class plays a large role in the opportunities and resources that will be available to a child. Working-class children often grow up at a disadvantage with the schooling, communities, and level of parental attention available compared to those from the middle-class or upper-class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Also, lower working-class families do not get the kind of networking that the middle and upper classes do through helpful family members, friends, and community individuals or groups as well as various professionals or experts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles">Styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parenting_styles" title="Parenting styles">Parenting styles</a></div>
<p>A parenting style is indicative of the overall emotional climate in the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spera_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spera-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Developmental_psychology" title="Developmental psychology">Developmental psychologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Diana_Baumrind" title="Diana Baumrind">Diana Baumrind</a> identified three main parenting styles in early <a href="/wiki/Child_development" title="Child development">child development</a>: authoritative, authoritarian, and permissive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (October 2021)">excessive citations</span></a></i>]</sup> These parenting styles were later expanded to four to include an uninvolved style. These four styles involve combinations of acceptance and responsiveness, and also involve demand and control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santrock_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santrock-19">[19]</a></sup> Research<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> has found that parenting style is significantly related to a child's subsequent mental health and well-being. In particular, authoritative parenting is positively related to mental health and satisfaction with life, and authoritarian parenting is negatively related to these variables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> With authoritarian and permissive parenting on opposite sides of the spectrum, most conventional modern models of parenting fall somewhere in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritative_parenting">Authoritative parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Authoritative parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd>Described by Baumrind as the "just right" style, it combines medium level demands on the child and a medium level responsiveness from the parents. Authoritative parents rely on positive reinforcement and infrequent use of punishment. Parents are more aware of a child's feelings and capabilities and support the development of a child's autonomy within reasonable limits. There is a give-and-take atmosphere involved in parent-child communication, and both control and support are balanced. Some research has shown that this style of parenting is more beneficial than the too-hard authoritarian style or the too-soft permissive style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> These children score higher in terms of competence, mental health, and social development than those raised in permissive, authoritarian, or neglectful homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarian_parenting">Authoritarian parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Authoritarian parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd>Authoritarian parents are very rigid and strict. High demands are placed on the child, but there is little responsiveness to them. Parents who practice authoritarian-style parenting have a non-negotiable set of rules and expectations strictly enforced and require rigid obedience. When the rules are not followed, punishment is often used to promote and ensure future compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> There is usually no explanation of punishment except that the child is in trouble for breaking a rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOI_10.1177/0192513X08322933_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOI_10.1177/0192513X08322933-28">[28]</a></sup> This parenting style is strongly associated with <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">corporal punishment</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Spanking" title="Spanking">spanking</a>. A typical response to a child’s question of authority would be, "because I said so." This type of parenting seems to be seen more often in working-class families than in the middle class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1983, Diana Baumrind found that children raised in an authoritarian-style home were less cheerful, moodier, and more vulnerable to stress. In many cases, these children also demonstrated passive hostility. This parenting style can negatively impact the educational success and career path, while a firm and reassuring parenting style impact positively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Permissive_parenting">Permissive parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Permissive parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd>Permissive parenting has become a more popular parenting method for middle-class families than working-class families roughly since the end of WWII.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> In these settings, a child's freedom and autonomy are highly valued, and parents rely primarily on reasoning and explanation. Parents are undemanding, and thus there tends to be little if any punishment or explicit rules in this parenting style. These parents say that their children are free from external constraints and tend to be highly responsive to whatever it is that the child wants. Children of permissive parents are generally happy but sometimes show low levels of self-control and self-reliance because they lack structure at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uninvolved_parenting">Uninvolved parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Uninvolved parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd>An uninvolved or neglectful parenting style is when parents are often emotionally or physically absent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOI:10.1080/01494920802010140_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOI:10.1080/01494920802010140-34">[34]</a></sup> They have little to no expectations from the child and regularly have no communication. They are not responsive to a child's needs and have little to no behavioral expectations. If present, they may provide what the child needs for survival with little to no engagement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOI:10.1080/01494920802010140_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-DOI:10.1080/01494920802010140-34">[34]</a></sup> There is often a large gap between parents and children with this parenting style.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (July 2018)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> Children with little or no communication with their own parents tend to be victimized by other children and may exhibit deviant behavior themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi_10.1177/1077559509347012_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi_10.1177/1077559509347012-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Children of uninvolved parents suffer in <a href="/wiki/Social_competence" title="Social competence">social competence</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_performance" title="Academic performance">academic performance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychosocial_development" title="Psychosocial development">psychosocial development</a>, and problematic behavior.</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intrusive_parenting">Intrusive parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Intrusive parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dd>Intrusive parenting is when parents use "parental control and inhibition of adolescents’ thoughts, feelings, and emotional expression through the use of love withdrawal, guilt induction, and manipulative tactics." It can disturb the adolescents' development and growth period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Children, especially adolescents, can be victims because they are "unassertive, avoid confrontation, are eager to please others, and suffer from low self-esteem."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Intrusive parents may try to set unrealistic expectations on their children, like enrolling them into more extracurricular activities or enrolling them into certain classes that aren't their child's passion. They may compare their children to others, like friends and family, and also force their child to be codependent--to a point where the children feel unprepared when they go into the world. Research has shown that this parenting style can lead to "greater under-eating behaviors, risky cyber behaviors, substance use, and depressive symptoms among adolescents."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-39">[39]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Father_and_child.jpg/220px-Father_and_child.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Father_and_child.jpg/330px-Father_and_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Father_and_child.jpg/440px-Father_and_child.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Father_and_child.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A father and son</div></div></div>
<p>A parenting practice is a specific behavior that a parent uses in raising a child. These practices are used to socialize children. Kuppens et al. found that "researchers have identified overarching parenting dimensions that reflect similar parenting practices, mostly by modeling the relationships among these parenting practices using factor analytic techniques."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> For example, many parents read aloud to their offspring in the hopes of supporting their linguistic and intellectual development. In cultures with strong oral traditions, such as Indigenous American communities and New Zealand Maori communities, <a href="/wiki/Storytelling#storytelling_in_indigenous_cultures" title="Storytelling">storytelling</a> is a critical parenting practice for children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inge_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-inge-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Parenting practices reflect the cultural understanding of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day-Look_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day-Look-43">[43]</a></sup> Parents in individualistic countries like Germany spend more time engaged in <a href="/wiki/Face-to-face_interaction" title="Face-to-face interaction">face-to-face interaction</a> with babies and more time talking to the baby about the baby. Parents in more communal cultures, such as West African cultures, spend more time talking to the baby about other people and more time with the baby facing outwards so that the baby sees what the mother sees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day-Look_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Day-Look-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skills_and_behaviors">Skills and behaviors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Skills and behaviors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Parenting skills and behaviors assist parents in leading children into healthy adulthood and development of the child's social skills. The cognitive potential, social skills, and behavioral functioning a child acquires during the early years are positively correlated with the quality of their interactions with their parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Canadian Council on Learning, children benefit (or avoid poor developmental outcomes) when their parents:
</p>
<ol><li>Communicate truthfully about events: Authenticity from parents who explain can help their children understand what happened and how they are involved;</li>
<li>Maintain consistency: Parents that regularly institute routines can see benefits in their children's behavioral patterns;</li>
<li>Utilize resources available to them, reaching out into the community and building a <a href="/wiki/It_takes_a_village" title="It takes a village">supportive social network</a>;</li>
<li>Take an interest in their child's educational and early developmental needs (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Play_(activity)" title="Play (activity)">Play</a> that enhances socialization, autonomy, cohesion, calmness, and trust.); and</li>
<li>Keep open communication lines about what their child is seeing, learning, and doing, and how those things are affecting them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Parenting skills are widely thought to be naturally present in parents; however, there is substantial evidence to the contrary. Those who come from a negative or vulnerable childhood environment frequently (and often unintentionally) mimic their parents' behavior during interactions with their own children. Parents with an inadequate understanding of developmental milestones may also demonstrate problematic parenting. Parenting practices are of particular importance during marital transitions like separation, divorce, and remarriage;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> if children fail to adequately adjust to these changes, they are at risk of negative outcomes (e.g. increased rule-breaking behavior, problems with peer relationships, and increased emotional difficulties).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research classifies competence and skills required in parenting as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Parent-child relationship skills: quality time spent, positive communications, and delighted show of affection.</li>
<li>Encouraging desirable behavior: praise and encouragement, nonverbal attention, facilitating engaging activities.</li>
<li>Teaching skills and behaviors: being a good example, <a href="/wiki/Learning#Incidental_learning" title="Learning">incidental teaching</a>, human communication of the skill with role-playing and other methods, communicating logical incentives and consequences.</li>
<li>Managing misbehavior: establishing firm ground rules and limits, directing discussion, providing clear and calm instructions, communicating and enforcing appropriate consequences, using restrictive tactics like quiet time and time out with an authoritative stance rather than an authoritarian one.</li>
<li>Anticipating and planning: advanced planning and preparation for readying the child for challenges, finding out engaging and age-appropriate developmental activities, preparing the token economy for self-management practice with guidance, holding follow-up discussions, identifying possible negative developmental trajectories.</li>
<li>Self-regulation skills: monitoring behaviors (own and children's),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> setting developmentally appropriate goals, evaluating strengths and weaknesses and setting practice tasks, monitoring and preventing internalizing and externalizing behaviors.</li>
<li>Mood and coping skills: reframing and discouraging unhelpful thoughts (diversions, goal orientation, and <a href="/wiki/Mindfulness" title="Mindfulness">mindfulness</a>), stress and tension management (<a href="/wiki/Parenting_stress" title="Parenting stress">own</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stress_in_early_childhood" title="Stress in early childhood">children's</a>), developing personal coping statements and plans for high-risk situations, building mutual respect and consideration between members of the family through collaborative activities and rituals.</li>
<li>Partner support skills: improving personal communication, giving and receiving constructive feedback and support, avoiding <a href="/wiki/Triangulation_(psychology)" title="Triangulation (psychology)">negative</a> family interaction styles, supporting and finding <a href="/wiki/Hope#In_psychology" title="Hope">hope</a> in problems for adaptation, leading collaborative problem solving, promoting relationship happiness and cordiality.</li></ul>
<p>Consistency is considered the “backbone” of positive parenting skills and “overprotection” the weakness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parent_training">Parent training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Parent training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Parent psychosocial health can have a significant impact on the parent-child relationship. Group-based <a href="/wiki/Parent_education_program" title="Parent education program">parent training and education programs</a> have proven to be effective at improving short-term psychosocial well-being for parents. There are many different types of training parents can take to support their parenting skills. Some groups include Parent-Child Interaction Therapy (PCIT), Parents Management Training (PMT), Positive Parenting Program (Triple P), The Incredible Years, and Behavioral and Emotional Skills Training (BEST). <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> PCIT works with both parents and children in teaching skills to interact more positively and productive. PMT is focused for children aged 3-13, in which parents are the main trainee. They are taught skills to help deal with challenging behaviors from their children. Triple P focus on equipping parents with the information they need to increase confidence and self-sufficiency in managing their children's behavior. The Incredible Years focuses in age infancy-age 12, in which they are broken into small-group-based training in different areas. BEST introduces effective behavior management techniques in one day rather than over the course of a few weeks. Courses are offered to families based on effective training to support additional needs, behavioral guidelines, communication and many others to give guidance throughout learning how to be a parent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_values">Cultural values</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cultural values">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Parents around the world want what they believe is best for their children. However, parents in different cultures have different ideas of what is best.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup> For example, parents in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter%E2%80%93gatherer" title="Hunter–gatherer">hunter–gatherer</a> societies or those who survive through <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence agriculture</a> are likely to promote practical survival skills from a young age. Many such cultures begin teaching children to use sharp tools, including knives, before their first birthdays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In some Indigenous American communities, <a href="/wiki/Child_work_in_indigenous_American_cultures" title="Child work in indigenous American cultures">child work</a> provides children the opportunity to absorb cultural values of collaborative participation and <a href="/wiki/Prosocial_behavior" title="Prosocial behavior">prosocial behavior</a> through <a href="/wiki/Observational_learning#Indigenous_communities_of_the_Americas" title="Observational learning">observation</a> and activity alongside adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inge_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-inge-41">[41]</a></sup> These communities value respect, participation, and non-interference, the Cherokee principle of respecting autonomy by withholding unsolicited advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Indigenous American parents also try to encourage curiosity in their children via a permissive parenting style that enables them to explore and <a href="/wiki/Observational_learning" title="Observational learning">learn through observation</a> of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inge_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-inge-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Differences in cultural values cause parents to interpret the same behaviors in different ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup> For instance, European Americans prize intellectual understanding, especially in a narrow "book learning" sense, and believe that asking questions is a sign of intelligence. Italian parents value social and emotional competence and believe that curiosity demonstrates good interpersonal skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup> Dutch parents, however, value independence, long attention spans, and predictability; in their eyes, asking questions is a negative behavior, signifying a lack of independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even so, parents around the world share specific prosocial behavioral goals for their children. Hispanic parents value respect and emphasize putting family above the individual. Parents in East Asia prize order in the household above all else. In some cases, this gives rise to high levels of psychological control and even manipulation on the part of the head of the household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kipsigis_people" title="Kipsigis people">Kipsigis people</a> of Kenya value children who are innovative and wield that intelligence responsibly and helpfully—a behavior they call <i>ng/om</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup> Other cultures, such as Sweden and Spain, value sociable and happiness as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Day_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-Day-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_American_cultures">Indigenous American cultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Indigenous American cultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baby_got_Back.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Baby_got_Back.jpg/220px-Baby_got_Back.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Baby_got_Back.jpg/330px-Baby_got_Back.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Baby_got_Back.jpg/440px-Baby_got_Back.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baby_got_Back.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Baby on back in Lima, Peru</div></div></div>
<p>It is common for parents in many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the americas">Indigenous American communities</a> to use different parenting tools such as storytelling —like myths— <i>Consejos</i> (Spanish for "advice"), educational teasing, nonverbal communication, and observational learning to teach their children important values and life lessons.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Storytelling#Storytelling_in_indigenous_cultures" title="Storytelling">Storytelling</a> is a way for Indigenous American children to learn about their identity, community, and cultural history. Indigenous myths and folklore often personify animals and objects, reaffirming the belief that everything possesses a soul and deserves respect. These stories also help preserve the language and are used to reflect certain values or cultural histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Consejo</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Narrative#In_cultural_storytelling" title="Narrative">narrative</a> form of advice-giving. Rather than directly telling the child what to do in a particular situation, the parent might instead tell a story about a similar situation. The main character in the story is intended to help the child see their decision's implications without directly deciding for them; this teaches the child to be decisive and independent while still providing some guidance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The playful form of <a href="/wiki/Teasing#Teasing_in_Indigenous_American_Communities" title="Teasing">teasing</a> is a parenting method used in some Indigenous American communities to keep children out of danger and guide their behavior. This parenting strategy utilizes stories, fabrications, or empty threats to guide children in making safe, intelligent decisions. For example, a parent may tell a child that there is a monster that jumps on children's backs if they walk alone at night. This explanation can help keep the child safe because instilling that fear creates greater awareness and lessens the likelihood that they will wander alone into trouble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In Navajo families, a child's development is partly focused on the importance of "respect" for all things. "Respect" consists of recognizing the significance of one's relationship with other things and people in the world. Children largely learn about this concept via <a href="/wiki/Nonverbal_communication#Children's_Learning_in_Indigenous_American_Communities" title="Nonverbal communication">nonverbal communication</a> between parents and other family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Source_1996_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Source_1996-61">[61]</a></sup> For example, children are initiated at an early age into the practice of an early morning run under any weather conditions. On this run, the community uses humor and laughter with each other, without directly including the child—who may not wish to get up early and run—to encourage the child to participate and become an active member of the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Source_1996_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Source_1996-61">[61]</a></sup> Parents also promote participation in the morning runs by placing their child in the snow and having them stay longer if they protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Source_1996_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Source_1996-61">[61]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo,_New_Mexico,_making_pottery,_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2286" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg/220px-Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg/330px-Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg/440px-Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo%2C_New_Mexico%2C_making_pottery%2C_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indians_of_Santa_Clara_Pueblo,_New_Mexico,_making_pottery,_1916_-_NARA_-_519165.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indians of Santa Clara Pueblo, New Mexico, making pottery, 1916</div></div></div>
<p>Indigenous American parents often <a href="/wiki/Child_integration" title="Child integration">incorporate children</a> into everyday life, including adult activities, allowing the child to <a href="/wiki/Observational_learning#Indigenous_communities_of_the_Americas" title="Observational learning">learn through observation</a>. This practice is known as LOPI, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Learning_by_Observing_and_Pitching_In&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Learning by Observing and Pitching In (page does not exist)">Learning by Observing and Pitching In</a>, where children are integrated into all types of mature daily activities and encouraged to observe and contribute in the community. This inclusion as a parenting tool promotes both community participation and learning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>One notable example appears in some Mayan communities: young girls are not permitted around the hearth for an extended period of time, since corn is sacred. Although this is an exception to their cultural preference for incorporating children into activities, including cooking, it is a strong example of observational learning. Mayan girls can only watch their mothers making tortillas for a few minutes at a time, but the sacredness of the activity captures their interest. They will then go and practice their mother's movements on other objects, such as kneading thin pieces of plastic like a tortilla. From this practice, when a girl comes of age, she is able to sit down and make tortillas without having ever received any explicit verbal instruction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigrants_in_the_United_States:_Ethnic-racial_socialization">Immigrants in the United States: Ethnic-racial socialization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Immigrants in the United States: Ethnic-racial socialization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to the increasing racial and ethnic diversity in the United States, ethnic-racial socialization research has gained some attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Parental ethnic-racial socialization is a way of passing down cultural resources to support children of color's psychosocial wellness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> The goals of ethnic-racial socialization are: to pass on a positive view of one's ethnic group and to help children cope with racism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Through a meta-analysis of published research on ethnic-racial socialization, ethnic-racial socialization positively affects psychosocial well-being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> This meta-analytic review focuses on research relevant to four indicators of psychosocial skills and how they are influenced by developmental stage, race and ethnicity, research designs, and the differences between parent and child self-reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> The dimensions of ethnic-racial socialization that are considered when looking for correlations with psychosocial skills are cultural socialization, preparation for bias, promotion of mistrust, and egalitarianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ethnic-racial socialization dimensions are defined as follows: cultural socialization is the process of passing down cultural customs, preparation for bias ranges from positive or negative reactions to racism and discrimination, promotion of mistrust conditions synergy when dealing with other races, and egalitarianism puts similarities between races first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Psychosocial competencies are defined as follows: self-perceptions involve perceived beliefs of academic and social capabilities, interpersonal relationships deal with the quality of relationships, externalizing behaviors deal with observable troublesome behavior, and internalizing behavior deals with emotional intelligence regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-7"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> The multiple ways these domains and competencies interact show small correlations between ethnic-racial socialization and psychosocial wellness, but this parenting practice needs further research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-8"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>This meta-analysis showed that developmental stages affect how children perceived ethnic-racial socialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-9"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Cultural socialization practices appear to affect children similarly across developmental stages except for preparation for bias and promotion of mistrust which are encouraged for older-aged children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> Existing research shows ethnic-racial socialization serves African Americans positively against discrimination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> Cross-sectional studies were predicted to have greater effect sizes because correlations are inflated in these kinds of studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-70">[70]</a></sup> Parental reports of ethnic-racial socialization influence are influenced by “intentions,” so child reports tend to be more accurate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among other conclusions derived from this meta-analysis, cultural socialization and self-perceptions had a small positive correlation. Cultural socialization and promotion of mistrust had a small negative correlation, and interpersonal relationships positively impacted cultural socialization and preparation for bias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-10"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> In regard to developmental stages, ethnic-racial socialization had a small but positive correlation with self-perceptions during childhood and early adolescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-11"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Based on study designs, there were no significant differences, meaning that cross-sectional studies and longitudinal studies both showed small positive correlations between ethnic-racial socialization and self-perceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-12"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Reporter differences between parents and children showed positive correlations between ethnic-racial socialization when associated with internalizing behavior and interpersonal relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-13"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> These two correlations showed a greater effect size with child reports compared to parent reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-14"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The meta-analysis on previous research shows only correlations, so there is a need for experimental studies that can show causation amongst the different domains and dimensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-15"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> Children's behavior and adaptation to this behavior may indicate a bidirectional effect that can also be addressed by an experimental study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-16"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> There is evidence to show that ethnic-racial socialization can help children of color obtain social-emotional skills that can help them navigate through racism and discrimination, but further research needs to be done to increase the generalizability of existing research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_64-17"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Across_the_lifespan">Across the lifespan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Across the lifespan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-pregnancy">Pre-pregnancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pre-pregnancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_care" title="Prenatal care">Prenatal care</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">Family planning</a> is the decision-making process surrounding whether to become parents or not, and when the right time would be, including planning, preparing, and gathering resources. Prospective parents may assess (among other matters) whether they have access to sufficient financial resources, whether their family situation is stable, and whether they want to undertake the responsibility of raising a child. Worldwide, about 40% of all pregnancies are not planned, and more than 30 million babies are born each year as a result of unplanned pregnancies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">Reproductive health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pre-conception_counseling" title="Pre-conception counseling">preconception care</a> affect pregnancy, reproductive success, and the physical and mental health of both mother and child. A woman who is <a href="/wiki/Underweight" title="Underweight">underweight</a>, whether due to poverty, <a href="/wiki/Eating_disorder" title="Eating disorder">eating disorders</a>, or illness, is less likely to have a healthy pregnancy and give birth to a healthy baby than a woman who is healthy. Similarly, a woman who is obese has a higher risk of difficulties, including <a href="/wiki/Gestational_diabetes" title="Gestational diabetes">gestational diabetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72">[72]</a></sup>  Other health problems, such as <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infections</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron-deficiency_anemia" title="Iron-deficiency anemia">iron-deficiency anemia</a>, can be detected and corrected before conception.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pregnancy_and_prenatal_parenting">Pregnancy and prenatal parenting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pregnancy and prenatal parenting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">Pregnancy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg"><img alt="A pregnant woman floats in the corner of a swimming pool" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1283" data-file-width="2347" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg/220px-Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg/330px-Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg/440px-Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pregnant_hot_tub.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pregnant women and their unborn children benefit from moderate exercise, sufficient sleep, and high-quality nutrition.</div></div></div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a>, the unborn child is affected by many decisions made by the parents, particularly choices linked to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyle</a>. The health, activity level, and nutrition available to the mother can affect the child's development before <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">birth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72">[72]</a></sup> Some mothers, especially in relatively wealthy countries, overeat and spend <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">too much time resting</a>. Other mothers, especially if they are poor or <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">abused</a>, may be <a href="/wiki/Overwork" title="Overwork">overworked</a> and may not be able to eat enough, or may not be able to afford healthful foods with sufficient iron, vitamins, and protein, for the unborn child to develop properly.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newborns_and_infants">Newborns and infants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Newborns and infants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">Infant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="906" data-file-width="573" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg/200px-Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg/300px-Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg/400px-Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blake_Infant_Joy_Copy_AA_25.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mother wishes joy towards her child in <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>'s poem "<a href="/wiki/Infant_Joy" title="Infant Joy">Infant Joy</a>". This copy, Copy AA, was printed and painted in 1826, is currently held by the <a href="/wiki/Fitzwilliam_Museum" title="Fitzwilliam Museum">Fitzwilliam Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newborn" title="Newborn">Newborn</a> parenting is where the responsibilities of parenthood begin. A newborn's basic needs are food, sleep, comfort, and cleaning, which the parent provides. An infant's only form of communication is crying, and attentive parents will begin to recognize different types of <a href="/wiki/Crying" title="Crying">crying</a> each of which represents different needs such as hunger, discomfort, boredom, or loneliness. Newborns and young infants require feedings every few hours, which is disruptive to adult <a href="/wiki/Sleep_cycle#Stages_of_sleep" title="Sleep cycle">sleep cycles</a>. They respond enthusiastically to soft stroking, cuddling, and caressing. Gentle rocking back and forth often calms a crying infant, as do massages and warm baths. Newborns may comfort themselves by sucking their thumb or by using a <a href="/wiki/Pacifier" title="Pacifier">pacifier</a>. The need to suckle is instinctive and allows newborns to feed. <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">Breastfeeding</a> is the recommended method of feeding by all major infant health organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAP_Policy_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAP_Policy-74">[74]</a></sup> If breastfeeding is not possible or desired, bottle feeding is a common alternative. Other alternatives include feeding breastmilk or formula with a cup, spoon, feeding syringe, or nursing supplement.
</p><p>The forming of <a href="/wiki/Attachment_in_children" title="Attachment in children">attachments</a> is considered the foundation of the infant's capacity to form and conduct relationships throughout life. Attachment is not the same as love or affection, although they often go together. Attachments develop immediately, and a lack of attachment or a seriously disrupted attachment has the potential to cause severe damage to a child's health and well-being. Physically, one may not see symptoms or indications of a disorder, but the child may be affected emotionally. Studies show that children with secure attachments have the ability to form successful relationships, express themselves on an interpersonal basis, and have higher self-esteem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Conversely children who have neglectful or emotionally unavailable caregivers can exhibit behavioral problems such as post-traumatic stress disorder or <a href="/wiki/Oppositional_defiant_disorder" title="Oppositional defiant disorder">oppositional defiant disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Oppositional-defiant disorder is a pattern of disobedient and rebellious behavior toward authority figures.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toddlers">Toddlers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Toddlers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">Toddler</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1271" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png/220px-Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png/330px-Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png/440px-Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maud_Humphrey_-_Maternal_cares.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting by Maud Humphrey of a child at a small table with dolls and toy china</div></div></div>
<p>Toddlers are small children between 12 and 36 months old who are much more active than infants and become challenged with learning how to do simple tasks by themselves. At this stage, parents are heavily involved in showing the small child how to do things rather than just doing things for them; it is normal for the <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">toddler</a> to mimic the parents. Toddlers need help to build their vocabulary, increase their communication skills, and manage their emotions. Toddlers will also begin to understand social etiquette, such as being polite and taking turns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parenting.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Parenting.JPG/220px-Parenting.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Parenting.JPG/330px-Parenting.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Parenting.JPG/440px-Parenting.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parenting.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A father and daughter in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trivandrum" title="Trivandrum">Trivandrum</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div></div></div>
<p>Toddlers are very curious about the world around them and are eager to explore it. They seek greater independence and responsibility and may become frustrated when things do not go the way that they want or expect. <a href="/wiki/Tantrum" title="Tantrum">Tantrums</a> begin at this stage, which is sometimes referred to as the 'Terrible Twos'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Tantrums are often caused by the child's frustration over the particular situation, and are sometimes caused, simply because they are not able to communicate properly. Parents of toddlers are expected to help guide and teach the child, establish basic routines (such as washing hands before meals or brushing teeth before bed), and increase the child's responsibilities. It is also normal for toddlers to be frequently frustrated. It is an essential step to their development. They will learn through experience, trial, and error. This means that they need to experience being frustrated when something does not work for them in order to move on to the next stage. When the toddler is frustrated, they will often misbehave with actions like screaming, hitting or biting. Parents need to be careful when reacting to such behaviors; giving threats or punishments is usually not helpful and might only make the situation worse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>  Research groups led by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Schechter" title="Daniel Schechter">Daniel Schechter</a>, Alytia Levendosky, and others have shown that parents with histories of maltreatment and violence exposure often have difficulty helping their toddlers and preschool-age children with the very same emotionally dysregulated behaviors which can remind traumatized parents of their adverse experiences and associated mental states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regarding <a href="/wiki/Sex_differences_in_humans#Sociology" title="Sex differences in humans">gender differences</a> in parenting, data from the US in 2014 states that, on an average day, among adults living in households with children under age 6, women spent 1.0 hours providing physical care (such as bathing or feeding a child) to household children. By contrast, men spent 23 minutes providing physical care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child">Child</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Child">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">Child</a>, <a href="/wiki/Early_childhood" title="Early childhood">Early childhood</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childhood" title="Childhood">Childhood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Preadolescence" title="Preadolescence">Preadolescence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2954" data-file-width="2141" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg/220px-Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg/330px-Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg/440px-Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miryam_Sidrensky_running_with_her_daughter.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sprint_(running)" title="Sprint (running)">Sprinter</a> <a href="/wiki/Miriam_Siderenski" title="Miriam Siderenski">Miriam Siderenski</a> running alongside her daughter.</div></div></div>
<p>Younger children start to become more independent and begin to build friendships. They are able to reason and can make their own decisions in many hypothetical situations. Young children demand constant attention but gradually learn how to deal with boredom and begin to be able to play independently. They enjoy helping and also feeling useful and capable. Parents can assist their children by encouraging social interactions and modeling proper social behaviors. A large part of learning in the early years comes from being involved in activities and household duties. Parents who observe their children in play or join with them in child-driven play have the opportunity to glimpse into their children's world, learn to communicate more effectively with their children, and are given another setting to offer gentle, nurturing guidance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAP_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAP-84">[84]</a></sup> Parents also teach their children health, hygiene, and eating habits through instruction and by example.
</p><p>Parents are expected to make decisions about their child's <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">education</a>. Parenting styles in this area diverge greatly at this stage, with some parents they choose to become heavily involved in arranging organized activities and early learning programs. Other parents choose to let the child develop with few organized activities.
</p><p>Children begin to learn responsibility and consequences for their actions with parental assistance. Some parents provide a small allowance that increases with age to help teach children the value of money and how to be responsible.
</p><p>Parents who are consistent and fair with their <a href="/wiki/Child_discipline" title="Child discipline">discipline</a>, who openly communicate and offer explanations to their children, and who do not neglect the needs of their children in any way often find they have fewer problems with their children as they mature.
</p><p>When child conduct problems are encountered, behavioral and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">cognitive-behavioral</a> group-based parenting interventions have been found to be effective at improving child conduct, parenting skills, and parental mental health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adolescents">Adolescents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Adolescents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Parenting%22" rel="nofollow">"Parenting"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Parenting%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Parenting%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Parenting%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Parenting%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Parenting%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2018</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">Adolescence</a></div>
<p>Parents often feel isolated and alone when parenting <a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">adolescents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Adolescence can be a time of high <a href="/wiki/Risk" title="Risk">risk</a> for children, where newfound freedoms can result in decisions that drastically open up or close off life opportunities. There are also large changes that occur in the <a class="external text" href="https://www.talkingtoteens.com/about-your-teenager/" rel="nofollow">brain during adolescence</a>; the emotional center of the brain is now fully developed, but the rational frontal cortex hasn't matured fully and still is not able to keep all of those emotions in check.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Adolescents tend to increase the amount of time spent with peers of the opposite gender; however, they still maintain the amount of time spent with those of the same gender—and do this by decreasing the amount of time spent with their parents.
</p><p>Although adolescents look to <a href="/wiki/Peer_group" title="Peer group">peers</a> and adults outside the family for guidance and models for how to behave, parents can remain influential in their development. Studies have shown that parents can have a significant impact, for instance, on how much teens drink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>During adolescence children begin to form their <a href="/wiki/Identity_(social_science)" title="Identity (social science)">identity</a> and start to test and develop the interpersonal and occupational roles that they will assume as adults. Therefore, it is important that parents treat them as young adults. Parental issues at this stage of parenting include dealing with rebelliousness related to a greater desire to partake in risky behaviors. In order to prevent risky behaviors, it is important for the parents to build a trusting relationship with their children. This can be achieved through behavioral control, parental monitoring, consistent discipline, parental warmth and support, inductive reasoning, and strong parent-child communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>When a trusting relationship is built up, adolescents are more likely to approach their parents for help when faced with negative peer pressure. Helping children build a strong foundation will ultimately help them resist negative peer pressure. Not only will a positive relationship between adolescent and parent benefit when faced with peer pressure, it will help with identity-processing in early adolescents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dellmann-Jenkins_177–186_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dellmann-Jenkins_177–186-91">[91]</a></sup>
Research by Berzonsky et al. found that adolescents that were open and trusting of their parents were given more freedom and their parents were less likely to track them and control their behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adults">Adults</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Adults">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Parenting does not usually end when a child turns 18. Support may be needed in a child's life well beyond the adolescent years and can continue into middle and later adulthood. Parenting can be a lifelong process. Parents may provide financial support to their adult children, which can also include providing an inheritance after death. The life perspective and wisdom given by a parent can benefit their adult children in their own lives. Becoming a <a href="/wiki/Grandparent" title="Grandparent">grandparent</a> is another milestone and has many similarities with parenting. Roles can be reversed in some ways when adult children become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caregivers" title="Caregivers">caregivers</a> to their elderly parents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dellmann-Jenkins_177–186_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dellmann-Jenkins_177–186-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assistance">Assistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Assistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Child_care" title="Child care">Child care</a></div>
<p>Parents may receive assistance with caring for their children through child care programs.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Childbearing_and_happiness">Childbearing and happiness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Parenting&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Childbearing and happiness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Data from the <a href="/wiki/British_Household_Panel_Survey" title="British Household Panel Survey">British Household Panel Survey</a> and the German <a href="/wiki/Socio-Economic_Panel" title="Socio-Economic Panel">Socio-Economic Panel</a> suggests that having up to two children increases happiness in the years around the birth, and mostly only for those who have postponed childbearing. However, having a third child is not shown to increase happiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
